and issue a deprecation warning.\\n107: \\n108:     The decorator takes two arguments: a RemovedInDjangoXXWarning warning\\n109:     category and a list of parameter names that have been changed from\\n110:     positional-or-keyword to keyword-only, in their original positional order.\\n111: \\n112:     Works on both functions and methods. To apply to a class constructor,\\n113:     decorate its __init__() method. To apply to a staticmethod or classmethod,\\n114:     use @deprecate_posargs after @staticmethod or @classmethod.\\n115: \\n116:     Example: to deprecate passing option1 or option2 as posargs, change::\\n117: \\n118:         def some_func(request, option1, option2=True):\\n119:             ...\\n120: \\n121:     to::\\n122: \\n123:         @deprecate_posargs(RemovedInDjangoXXWarning, [\\\"option1\\\", \\\"option2\\\"])\\n124:         def some_func(request, *, option1, option2=True):\\n125:             ...\\n126: \\n127:     After the deprecation period, remove the decorator (but keep the ``*``)::\\n128: \\n129:         def some_func(request, *, option1, option2=True):\\n130:             ...\\n131: \\n132:     Caution: during the deprecation period, do not add any new *positional*\\n133:     parameters or change the remaining ones. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, every middleware will always have its\n431:    ``process_response`` method called, even if an earlier middleware\n432:    short-circuited by returning a response from its ``process_request``\n433:    method. {"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01Hddhyuy7Y5Zr6vf1cHNNkn","type":"tool_result","content":"{\"content\":\"1: ==========\\n2: Middleware\\n3: ==========\\n4: \\n5: Middleware is a framework of hooks into Django's request/response processing.\\n6: It's a light, low-level \\\"plugin\\\" system for globally altering Django's input\\n7: or output.\\n8: \\n9: Each middleware component is responsible for doing some specific function. For\\n10: example, Django includes a middleware component,\\n11: :class:`~django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware`, that\\n12: associates users with requests using sessions.\\n13: \\n14: This document explains how middleware works, how you activate middleware, and\\n15: how to write your own middleware. Django ships with some built-in middleware\\n16: you can use right out of the box. They're documented in the :doc:`built-in\\n17: middleware reference </ref/middleware>`.\\n18: \\n19: Writing your own middleware\\n20: ===========================\\n21: \\n22: A middleware factory is a callable that takes a ``get_response`` callable and\\n23: returns a middleware. A middleware is a callable that takes a request and\\n24: returns a response, just like a view.\\n25: \\n26: A middleware can be written as a function that looks like this::\\n27: \\n28:     def simple_middleware(get_response):\\n29:         # One-time configuration and initialization.\\n30: \\n31:         def middleware(request):\\n32:             # Code to be executed for each request before\\n33:             # the view (and later middleware) are called.\\n34: \\n35:             response = get_response(request)\\n36: \\n37:             # Code to be executed for each request/response after\\n38:             # the view is called.\\n39: \\n40:             return response\\n41: \\n42:         return middleware\\n43: \\n44: Or it can be written as a class whose instances are callable, like this::\\n45: \\n46:     class SimpleMiddleware:\\n47:         def __init__(self, get_response):\\n48:             self.get_response = get_response\\n49:             # One-time configuration and initialization.\\n50: \\n51:         def __call__(self, request):\\n52:             # Code to be executed for each request before\\n53:             # the view (and later middleware) are called.\\n54: \\n55:             response = self.get_response(request)\\n56: \\n57:             # Code to be executed for each request/response after\\n58:             # the view is called.\\n59: \\n60:             return response\\n61: \\n62: The ``get_response`` callable provided by Django might be the actual view (if\\n63: this is the last listed middleware) or it might be the next middleware in the\\n64: chain. The current middleware doesn't need to know or care what exactly it is,\\n65: just that it represents whatever comes next.\\n66: \\n67: The above is a slight simplification -- the ``get_response`` callable for the\\n68: last middleware in the chain won't be the actual view but rather a wrapper\\n69: method from the handler which takes care of applying :ref:`view middleware\\n70: <view-middleware>`, calling the view with appropriate URL arguments, and\\n71: applying :ref:`template-response <template-response-middleware>` and\\n72: :ref:`exception <exception-middleware>` middleware.\\n73: \\n74: Middleware can either support only synchronous Python (the default), only\\n75: asynchronous Python, or both. See :ref:`async-middleware` for details of how to\\n76: advertise what you support, and know what kind of request you are getting.\\n77: \\n78: Middleware can live anywhere on your Python path.\\n79: \\n80: ``__init__(get_response)``\\n81: --------------------------\\n82: \\n83: Middleware factories must accept a ``get_response`` argument. You can also\\n84: initialize some global state for the middleware. Keep in mind a couple of\\n85: caveats:\\n86: \\n87: * Django initializes your middleware with only the ``get_response`` argument,\\n88:   so you can't define ``__init__()`` as requiring any other arguments.\\n89: \\n90: * Unlike the ``__call__()`` method which is called once per request,\\n91:   ``__init__()`` is called only *once*, when the web server starts.\\n92: \\n93: Marking middleware as unused\\n94: ----------------------------\\n95: \\n96: It's sometimes useful to determine at startup time whether a piece of\\n97: middleware should be used. In these cases, your middleware's ``__init__()``\\n98: method may raise :exc:`~django.core.exceptions.MiddlewareNotUsed`. Django will\\n99: then remove that middleware from the middleware process and log a debug message\\n100: to the :ref:`django-request-logger` logger when :setting:`DEBUG` is ``True``.\\n101: \\n102: Activating middleware\\n103: =====================\\n104: \\n105: To activate a middleware component, add it to the :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` list in\\n106: your Django settings.\\n107: \\n108: In :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, each middleware component is represented by a string:\\n109: the full Python path to the middleware factory's class or function name. For\\n110: example, here's the default value created by :djadmin:`django-admin\\n111: startproject <startproject>`::\\n112: \\n113:     MIDDLEWARE = [\\n114:         \\\"django.middleware.security.SecurityMiddleware\\\",\\n115:         \\\"django.contrib.sessions.middleware.SessionMiddleware\\\",\\n116:         \\\"django.middleware.common.CommonMiddleware\\\",\\n117:         \\\"django.middleware.csrf.CsrfViewMiddleware\\\",\\n118:         \\\"django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware\\\",\\n119:         \\\"django.contrib.messages.middleware.MessageMiddleware\\\",\\n120:         \\\"django.middleware.clickjacking.XFrameOptionsMiddleware\\\",\\n121:     ]\\n122: \\n123: A Django installation doesn't require any middleware — :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`\\n124: can be empty, if you'd like — but it's strongly suggested that you at least use\\n125: :class:`~django.middleware.common.CommonMiddleware`.\\n126: \\n127: The order in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` matters because a middleware can depend on\\n128: other middleware. For instance,\\n129: :class:`~django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware` stores the\\n130: authenticated user in the session; therefore, it must run after\\n131: :class:`~django.contrib.sessions.middleware.SessionMiddleware`. See\\n132: :ref:`middleware-ordering` for some common hints about ordering of Django\\n133: middleware classes.\\n134: \\n135: Middleware order and layering\\n136: =============================\\n137: \\n138: During the request phase, before calling the view, Django applies middleware in\\n139: the order it's defined in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, top-down.\\n140: \\n141: You can think of it like an onion: each middleware class is a \\\"layer\\\" that\\n142: wraps the view, which is in the core of the onion. If the request passes\\n143: through all the layers of the onion (each one calls ``get_response`` to pass\\n144: the request in to the next layer), all the way to the view at the core, the\\n145: response will then pass through every layer (in reverse order) on the way back\\n146: out.\\n147: \\n148: If one of the layers decides to short-circuit and return a response without\\n149: ever calling its ``get_response``, none of the layers of the onion inside that\\n150: layer (including the view) will see the request or the response. The response\\n151: will only return through the same layers that the request passed in through.\\n152: \\n153: Other middleware hooks\\n154: ======================\\n155: \\n156: Besides the basic request/response middleware pattern described earlier, you\\n157: can add three other special methods to class-based middleware:\\n158: \\n159: .. _view-middleware:\\n160: \\n161: ``process_view()``\\n162: ------------------\\n163: \\n164: .. method:: process_view(request, view_func, view_args, view_kwargs)\\n165: \\n166: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``view_func`` is\\n167: the Python function that Django is about to use. (It's the actual function\\n168: object, not the name of the function as a string.) ``view_args`` is a list of\\n169: positional arguments that will be passed to the view, and ``view_kwargs`` is a\\n170: dictionary of keyword arguments that will be passed to the view. Neither\\n171: ``view_args`` nor ``view_kwargs`` include the first view argument\\n172: (``request``).\\n173: \\n174: ``process_view()`` is called just before Django calls the view.\\n175: \\n176: It should return either ``None`` or an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`\\n177: object. If it returns ``None``, Django will continue processing this request,\\n178: executing any other ``process_view()`` middleware and, then, the appropriate\\n179: view. If it returns an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object, Django won't\\n180: bother calling the appropriate view; it'll apply response middleware to that\\n181: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` and return the result.\\n182: \\n183: .. note::\\n184: \\n185:     Accessing :attr:`request.POST <django.http.HttpRequest.POST>` inside\\n186:     middleware before the view runs or in ``process_view()`` will prevent any\\n187:     view running after the middleware from being able to :ref:`modify the\\n188:     upload handlers for the request <modifying_upload_handlers_on_the_fly>`,\\n189:     and should normally be avoided.\\n190: \\n191:     The :class:`~django.middleware.csrf.CsrfViewMiddleware` class can be\\n192:     considered an exception, as it provides the\\n193:     :func:`~django.views.decorators.csrf.csrf_exempt` and\\n194:     :func:`~django.views.decorators.csrf.csrf_protect` decorators which allow\\n195:     views to explicitly control at what point the CSRF validation should occur.\\n196: \\n197: .. _exception-middleware:\\n198: \\n199: ``process_exception()``\\n200: -----------------------\\n201: \\n202: .. method:: process_exception(request, exception)\\n203: \\n204: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``exception`` is an\\n205: ``Exception`` object raised by the view function.\\n206: \\n207: Django calls ``process_exception()`` when a view raises an exception.\\n208: ``process_exception()`` should return either ``None`` or an\\n209: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object. If it returns an\\n210: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object, the template response and response\\n211: middleware will be applied and the resulting response returned to the\\n212: browser. Otherwise, :ref:`default exception handling <error-views>` kicks in.\\n213: \\n214: Again, middleware are run in reverse order during the response phase, which\\n215: includes ``process_exception``. If an exception middleware returns a response,\\n216: the ``process_exception`` methods of the middleware classes above that\\n217: middleware won't be called at all.\\n218: \\n219: .. _template-response-middleware:\\n220: \\n221: ``process_template_response()``\\n222: -------------------------------\\n223: \\n224: .. method:: process_template_response(request, response)\\n225: \\n226: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``response`` is\\n227: the :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` object (or equivalent)\\n228: returned by a Django view or by a middleware.\\n229: \\n230: ``process_template_response()`` is called just after the view has finished\\n231: executing, if the response instance has a ``render()`` method, indicating that\\n232: it is a :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` or equivalent.\\n233: \\n234: It must return a response object that implements a ``render`` method. It could\\n235: alter the given ``response`` by changing ``response.template_name`` and\\n236: ``response.context_data``, or it could create and return a brand-new\\n237: :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` or equivalent.\\n238: \\n239: You don't need to explicitly render responses -- responses will be\\n240: automatically rendered once all template response middleware has been\\n241: called.\\n242: \\n243: Middleware are run in reverse order during the response phase, which\\n244: includes ``process_template_response()``.\\n245: \\n246: Dealing with streaming responses\\n247: ================================\\n248: \\n249: Unlike :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`,\\n250: :class:`~django.http.StreamingHttpResponse` does not have a ``content``\\n251: attribute. As a result, middleware can no longer assume that all responses\\n252: will have a ``content`` attribute. If they need access to the content, they\\n253: must test for streaming responses and adjust their behavior accordingly::\\n254: \\n255:     if response.streaming:\\n256:         response.streaming_content = wrap_streaming_content(response.streaming_content)\\n257:     else:\\n258:         response.content = alter_content(response.content)\\n259: \\n260: .. note::\\n261: \\n262:     ``streaming_content`` should be assumed to be too large to hold in memory.\\n263:     Response middleware may wrap it in a new generator, but must not consume\\n264:     it. Wrapping is typically implemented as follows::\\n265: \\n266:         def wrap_streaming_content(content):\\n267:             for chunk in content:\\n268:                 yield alter_content(chunk)\\n269: \\n270: :class:`~django.http.StreamingHttpResponse` allows both synchronous and\\n271: asynchronous iterators. The wrapping function must match. Check\\n272: :attr:`StreamingHttpResponse.is_async\\n273: <django.http.StreamingHttpResponse.is_async>` if your middleware needs to\\n274: support both types of iterator.\\n275: \\n276: Exception handling\\n277: ==================\\n278: \\n279: Django automatically converts exceptions raised by the view or by middleware\\n280: into an appropriate HTTP response with an error status code. :ref:`Certain\\n281: exceptions <error-views>` are converted to 4xx status codes, while an unknown\\n282: exception is converted to a 500 status code.\\n283: \\n284: This conversion takes place before and after each middleware (you can think of\\n285: it as the thin film in between each layer of the onion), so that every\\n286: middleware can always rely on getting some kind of HTTP response back from\\n287: calling its ``get_response`` callable. Middleware don't need to worry about\\n288: wrapping their call to ``get_response`` in a ``try/except`` and handling an\\n289: exception that might have been raised by a later middleware or the view. Even\\n290: if the very next middleware in the chain raises an\\n291: :class:`~django.http.Http404` exception, for example, your middleware won't see\\n292: that exception; instead it will get an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`\\n293: object with a :attr:`~django.http.HttpResponse.status_code` of 404.\\n294: \\n295: You can set :setting:`DEBUG_PROPAGATE_EXCEPTIONS` to ``True`` to skip this\\n296: conversion and propagate exceptions upward.\\n297: \\n298: .. _async-middleware:\\n299: \\n300: Asynchronous support\\n301: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/django/django@202eca7795710c82cd64a248650afc64f905a17e/-/blob/docs/topics/http/middleware.txt%3FL1-300\"}"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"0e625766-dd17-43dd-9caa-0fcad3ad0d87","uuid":"b3a8e594-52ff-4c1b-b309-bdc890f262f6","tool_use_result":{"content":"{\"content\":\"1: ==========\\n2: Middleware\\n3: ==========\\n4: \\n5: Middleware is a framework of hooks into Django's request/response processing.\\n6: It's a light, low-level \\\"plugin\\\" system for globally altering Django's input\\n7: or output.\\n8: \\n9: Each middleware component is responsible for doing some specific function. For\\n10: example, Django includes a middleware component,\\n11: :class:`~django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware`, that\\n12: associates users with requests using sessions.\\n13: \\n14: This document explains how middleware works, how you activate middleware, and\\n15: how to write your own middleware. Django ships with some built-in middleware\\n16: you can use right out of the box. They're documented in the :doc:`built-in\\n17: middleware reference </ref/middleware>`.\\n18: \\n19: Writing your own middleware\\n20: ===========================\\n21: \\n22: A middleware factory is a callable that takes a ``get_response`` callable and\\n23: returns a middleware. A middleware is a callable that takes a request and\\n24: returns a response, just like a view.\\n25: \\n26: A middleware can be written as a function that looks like this::\\n27: \\n28:     def simple_middleware(get_response):\\n29:         # One-time configuration and initialization.\\n30: \\n31:         def middleware(request):\\n32:             # Code to be executed for each request before\\n33:             # the view (and later middleware) are called.\\n34: \\n35:             response = get_response(request)\\n36: \\n37:             # Code to be executed for each request/response after\\n38:             # the view is called.\\n39: \\n40:             return response\\n41: \\n42:         return middleware\\n43: \\n44: Or it can be written as a class whose instances are callable, like this::\\n45: \\n46:     class SimpleMiddleware:\\n47:         def __init__(self, get_response):\\n48:             self.get_response = get_response\\n49:             # One-time configuration and initialization.\\n50: \\n51:         def __call__(self, request):\\n52:             # Code to be executed for each request before\\n53:             # the view (and later middleware) are called.\\n54: \\n55:             response = self.get_response(request)\\n56: \\n57:             # Code to be executed for each request/response after\\n58:             # the view is called.\\n59: \\n60:             return response\\n61: \\n62: The ``get_response`` callable provided by Django might be the actual view (if\\n63: this is the last listed middleware) or it might be the next middleware in the\\n64: chain. The current middleware doesn't need to know or care what exactly it is,\\n65: just that it represents whatever comes next.\\n66: \\n67: The above is a slight simplification -- the ``get_response`` callable for the\\n68: last middleware in the chain won't be the actual view but rather a wrapper\\n69: method from the handler which takes care of applying :ref:`view middleware\\n70: <view-middleware>`, calling the view with appropriate URL arguments, and\\n71: applying :ref:`template-response <template-response-middleware>` and\\n72: :ref:`exception <exception-middleware>` middleware.\\n73: \\n74: Middleware can either support only synchronous Python (the default), only\\n75: asynchronous Python, or both. See :ref:`async-middleware` for details of how to\\n76: advertise what you support, and know what kind of request you are getting.\\n77: \\n78: Middleware can live anywhere on your Python path.\\n79: \\n80: ``__init__(get_response)``\\n81: --------------------------\\n82: \\n83: Middleware factories must accept a ``get_response`` argument. You can also\\n84: initialize some global state for the middleware. Keep in mind a couple of\\n85: caveats:\\n86: \\n87: * Django initializes your middleware with only the ``get_response`` argument,\\n88:   so you can't define ``__init__()`` as requiring any other arguments.\\n89: \\n90: * Unlike the ``__call__()`` method which is called once per request,\\n91:   ``__init__()`` is called only *once*, when the web server starts.\\n92: \\n93: Marking middleware as unused\\n94: ----------------------------\\n95: \\n96: It's sometimes useful to determine at startup time whether a piece of\\n97: middleware should be used. In these cases, your middleware's ``__init__()``\\n98: method may raise :exc:`~django.core.exceptions.MiddlewareNotUsed`. Django will\\n99: then remove that middleware from the middleware process and log a debug message\\n100: to the :ref:`django-request-logger` logger when :setting:`DEBUG` is ``True``.\\n101: \\n102: Activating middleware\\n103: =====================\\n104: \\n105: To activate a middleware component, add it to the :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` list in\\n106: your Django settings.\\n107: \\n108: In :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, each middleware component is represented by a string:\\n109: the full Python path to the middleware factory's class or function name. For\\n110: example, here's the default value created by :djadmin:`django-admin\\n111: startproject <startproject>`::\\n112: \\n113:     MIDDLEWARE = [\\n114:         \\\"django.middleware.security.SecurityMiddleware\\\",\\n115:         \\\"django.contrib.sessions.middleware.SessionMiddleware\\\",\\n116:         \\\"django.middleware.common.CommonMiddleware\\\",\\n117:         \\\"django.middleware.csrf.CsrfViewMiddleware\\\",\\n118:         \\\"django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware\\\",\\n119:         \\\"django.contrib.messages.middleware.MessageMiddleware\\\",\\n120:         \\\"django.middleware.clickjacking.XFrameOptionsMiddleware\\\",\\n121:     ]\\n122: \\n123: A Django installation doesn't require any middleware — :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`\\n124: can be empty, if you'd like — but it's strongly suggested that you at least use\\n125: :class:`~django.middleware.common.CommonMiddleware`.\\n126: \\n127: The order in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` matters because a middleware can depend on\\n128: other middleware. For instance,\\n129: :class:`~django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware` stores the\\n130: authenticated user in the session; therefore, it must run after\\n131: :class:`~django.contrib.sessions.middleware.SessionMiddleware`. See\\n132: :ref:`middleware-ordering` for some common hints about ordering of Django\\n133: middleware classes.\\n134: \\n135: Middleware order and layering\\n136: =============================\\n137: \\n138: During the request phase, before calling the view, Django applies middleware in\\n139: the order it's defined in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, top-down.\\n140: \\n141: You can think of it like an onion: each middleware class is a \\\"layer\\\" that\\n142: wraps the view, which is in the core of the onion. If the request passes\\n143: through all the layers of the onion (each one calls ``get_response`` to pass\\n144: the request in to the next layer), all the way to the view at the core, the\\n145: response will then pass through every layer (in reverse order) on the way back\\n146: out.\\n147: \\n148: If one of the layers decides to short-circuit and return a response without\\n149: ever calling its ``get_response``, none of the layers of the onion inside that\\n150: layer (including the view) will see the request or the response. The response\\n151: will only return through the same layers that the request passed in through.\\n152: \\n153: Other middleware hooks\\n154: ======================\\n155: \\n156: Besides the basic request/response middleware pattern described earlier, you\\n157: can add three other special methods to class-based middleware:\\n158: \\n159: .. _view-middleware:\\n160: \\n161: ``process_view()``\\n162: ------------------\\n163: \\n164: .. method:: process_view(request, view_func, view_args, view_kwargs)\\n165: \\n166: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``view_func`` is\\n167: the Python function that Django is about to use. (It's the actual function\\n168: object, not the name of the function as a string.) ``view_args`` is a list of\\n169: positional arguments that will be passed to the view, and ``view_kwargs`` is a\\n170: dictionary of keyword arguments that will be passed to the view. Neither\\n171: ``view_args`` nor ``view_kwargs`` include the first view argument\\n172: (``request``).\\n173: \\n174: ``process_view()`` is called just before Django calls the view.\\n175: \\n176: It should return either ``None`` or an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`\\n177: object. If it returns ``None``, Django will continue processing this request,\\n178: executing any other ``process_view()`` middleware and, then, the appropriate\\n179: view. If it returns an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object, Django won't\\n180: bother calling the appropriate view; it'll apply response middleware to that\\n181: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` and return the result.\\n182: \\n183: .. note::\\n184: \\n185:     Accessing :attr:`request.POST <django.http.HttpRequest.POST>` inside\\n186:     middleware before the view runs or in ``process_view()`` will prevent any\\n187:     view running after the middleware from being able to :ref:`modify the\\n188:     upload handlers for the request <modifying_upload_handlers_on_the_fly>`,\\n189:     and should normally be avoided.\\n190: \\n191:     The :class:`~django.middleware.csrf.CsrfViewMiddleware` class can be\\n192:     considered an exception, as it provides the\\n193:     :func:`~django.views.decorators.csrf.csrf_exempt` and\\n194:     :func:`~django.views.decorators.csrf.csrf_protect` decorators which allow\\n195:     views to explicitly control at what point the CSRF validation should occur.\\n196: \\n197: .. _exception-middleware:\\n198: \\n199: ``process_exception()``\\n200: -----------------------\\n201: \\n202: .. method:: process_exception(request, exception)\\n203: \\n204: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``exception`` is an\\n205: ``Exception`` object raised by the view function.\\n206: \\n207: Django calls ``process_exception()`` when a view raises an exception.\\n208: ``process_exception()`` should return either ``None`` or an\\n209: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object. If it returns an\\n210: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object, the template response and response\\n211: middleware will be applied and the resulting response returned to the\\n212: browser. Otherwise, :ref:`default exception handling <error-views>` kicks in.\\n213: \\n214: Again, middleware are run in reverse order during the response phase, which\\n215: includes ``process_exception``. If an exception middleware returns a response,\\n216: the ``process_exception`` methods of the middleware classes above that\\n217: middleware won't be called at all.\\n218: \\n219: .. _template-response-middleware:\\n220: \\n221: ``process_template_response()``\\n222: -------------------------------\\n223: \\n224: .. method:: process_template_response(request, response)\\n225: \\n226: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``response`` is\\n227: the :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` object (or equivalent)\\n228: returned by a Django view or by a middleware.\\n229: \\n230: ``process_template_response()`` is called just after the view has finished\\n231: executing, if the response instance has a ``render()`` method, indicating that\\n232: it is a :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` or equivalent.\\n233: \\n234: It must return a response object that implements a ``render`` method. It could\\n235: alter the given ``response`` by changing ``response.template_name`` and\\n236: ``response.context_data``, or it could create and return a brand-new\\n237: :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` or equivalent.\\n238: \\n239: You don't need to explicitly render responses -- responses will be\\n240: automatically rendered once all template response middleware has been\\n241: called.\\n242: \\n243: Middleware are run in reverse order during the response phase, which\\n244: includes ``process_template_response()``.\\n245: \\n246: Dealing with streaming responses\\n247: ================================\\n248: \\n249: Unlike :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`,\\n250: :class:`~django.http.StreamingHttpResponse` does not have a ``content``\\n251: attribute. As a result, middleware can no longer assume that all responses\\n252: will have a ``content`` attribute. If they need access to the content, they\\n253: must test for streaming responses and adjust their behavior accordingly::\\n254: \\n255:     if response.streaming:\\n256:         response.streaming_content = wrap_streaming_content(response.streaming_content)\\n257:     else:\\n258:         response.content = alter_content(response.content)\\n259: \\n260: .. note::\\n261: \\n262:     ``streaming_content`` should be assumed to be too large to hold in memory.\\n263:     Response middleware may wrap it in a new generator, but must not consume\\n264:     it. Wrapping is typically implemented as follows::\\n265: \\n266:         def wrap_streaming_content(content):\\n267:             for chunk in content:\\n268:                 yield alter_content(chunk)\\n269: \\n270: :class:`~django.http.StreamingHttpResponse` allows both synchronous and\\n271: asynchronous iterators. The wrapping function must match. Check\\n272: :attr:`StreamingHttpResponse.is_async\\n273: <django.http.StreamingHttpResponse.is_async>` if your middleware needs to\\n274: support both types of iterator.\\n275: \\n276: Exception handling\\n277: ==================\\n278: \\n279: Django automatically converts exceptions raised by the view or by middleware\\n280: into an appropriate HTTP response with an error status code. :ref:`Certain\\n281: exceptions <error-views>` are converted to 4xx status codes, while an unknown\\n282: exception is converted to a 500 status code.\\n283: \\n284: This conversion takes place before and after each middleware (you can think of\\n285: it as the thin film in between each layer of the onion), so that every\\n286: middleware can always rely on getting some kind of HTTP response back from\\n287: calling its ``get_response`` callable. Middleware don't need to worry about\\n288: wrapping their call to ``get_response`` in a ``try/except`` and handling an\\n289: exception that might have been raised by a later middleware or the view. Even\\n290: if the very next middleware in the chain raises an\\n291: :class:`~django.http.Http404` exception, for example, your middleware won't see\\n292: that exception; instead it will get an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`\\n293: object with a :attr:`~django.http.HttpResponse.status_code` of 404.\\n294: \\n295: You can set :setting:`DEBUG_PROPAGATE_EXCEPTIONS` to ``True`` to skip this\\n296: conversion and propagate exceptions upward.\\n297: \\n298: .. _async-middleware:\\n299: \\n300: Asynchronous support\\n301: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/django/django@202eca7795710c82cd64a248650afc64f905a17e/-/blob/docs/topics/http/middleware.txt%3FL1-300\"}","structuredContent":{"content":"1: ==========\n2: Middleware\n3: ==========\n4: \n5: Middleware is a framework of hooks into Django's request/response processing.\n6: It's a light, low-level \"plugin\" system for globally altering Django's input\n7: or output.\n8: \n9: Each middleware component is responsible for doing some specific function. See\n132: :ref:`middleware-ordering` for some common hints about ordering of Django\n133: middleware classes.\n134: \n135: Middleware order and layering\n136: =============================\n137: \n138: During the request phase, before calling the view, Django applies middleware in\n139: the order it's defined in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, top-down.\n140: \n141: You can think of it like an onion: each middleware class is a \"layer\" that\n142: wraps the view, which is in the core of the onion. If the request passes\n143: through all the layers of the onion (each one calls ``get_response`` to pass\n144: the request in to the next layer), all the way to the view at the core, the\n145: response will then pass through every layer (in reverse order) on the way back\n146: out.\n147: \n148: If one of the layers decides to short-circuit and return a response without\n149: ever calling its ``get_response``, none of the layers of the onion inside that\n150: layer (including the view) will see the request or the response. The response\n151: will only return through the same layers that the request passed in through.\n152: \n153: Other middleware hooks\n154: ======================\n155: \n156: Besides the basic request/response middleware pattern described earlier, you\n157: can add three other special methods to class-based middleware:\n158: \n159: .. _view-middleware:\n160: \n161: ``process_view()``\n162: ------------------\n163: \n164: .. method:: process_view(request, view_func, view_args, view_kwargs)\n165: \n166: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``view_func`` is\n167: the Python function that Django is about to use. (It's the actual function\n168: object, not the name of the function as a string.) ``view_args`` is a list of\n169: positional arguments that will be passed to the view, and ``view_kwargs`` is a\n170: dictionary of keyword arguments that will be passed to the view. Neither\n171: ``view_args`` nor ``view_kwargs`` include the first view argument\n172: (``request``).\n173: \n174: ``process_view()`` is called just before Django calls the view.\n175: \n176: It should return either ``None`` or an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`\n177: object. If it returns ``None``, Django will continue processing this request,\n178: executing any other ``process_view()`` middleware and, then, the appropriate\n179: view. If it returns an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object, Django won't\n180: bother calling the appropriate view; it'll apply response middleware to that\n181: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` and return the result.\n182: \n183: .. note::\n184: \n185:     Accessing :attr:`request.POST <django.http.HttpRequest.POST>` inside\n186:     middleware before the view runs or in ``process_view()`` will prevent any\n187:     view running after the middleware from being able to :ref:`modify the\n188:     upload handlers for the request <modifying_upload_handlers_on_the_fly>`,\n189:     and should normally be avoided.\n190: \n191:     The :class:`~django.middleware.csrf.CsrfViewMiddleware` class can be\n192:     considered an exception, as it provides the\n193:     :func:`~django.views.decorators.csrf.csrf_exempt` and\n194:     :func:`~django.views.decorators.csrf.csrf_protect` decorators which allow\n195:     views to explicitly control at what point the CSRF validation should occur.\n196: \n197: .. _exception-middleware:\n198: \n199: ``process_exception()``\n200: -----------------------\n201: \n202: .. method:: process_exception(request, exception)\n203: \n204: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``exception`` is an\n205: ``Exception`` object raised by the view function.\n206: \n207: Django calls ``process_exception()`` when a view raises an exception.\n208: ``process_exception()`` should return either ``None`` or an\n209: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object. If it returns an\n210: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object, the template response and response\n211: middleware will be applied and the resulting response returned to the\n212: browser. Otherwise, :ref:`default exception handling <error-views>` kicks in.\n213: \n214: Again, middleware are run in reverse order during the response phase, which\n215: includes ``process_exception``. If an exception middleware returns a response,\n216: the ``process_exception`` methods of the middleware classes above that\n217: middleware won't be called at all.\n218: \n219: .. _template-response-middleware:\n220: \n221: ``process_template_response()``\n222: -------------------------------\n223: \n224: .. method:: process_template_response(request, response)\n225: \n226: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``response`` is\n227: the :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` object (or equivalent)\n228: returned by a Django view or by a middleware.\n229: \n230: ``process_template_response()`` is called just after the view has finished\n231: executing, if the response instance has a ``render()`` method, indicating that\n232: it is a :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` or equivalent.\n233: \n234: It must return a response object that implements a ``render`` method. It could\n235: alter the given ``response`` by changing ``response.template_name`` and\n236: ``response.context_data``, or it could create and return a brand-new\n237: :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` or equivalent.\n238: \n239: You don't need to explicitly render responses -- responses will be\n240: automatically rendered once all template response middleware has been\n241: called.\n242: \n243: Middleware are run in reverse order during the response phase, which\n244: includes ``process_template_response()``.\n245: \n246: Dealing with streaming responses\n247: ================================\n248: \n249: Unlike :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`,\n250: :class:`~django.http.StreamingHttpResponse` does not have a ``content``\n251: attribute. As a result, middleware can no longer assume that all responses\n252: will have a ``content`` attribute. If they need access to the content, they\n253: must test for streaming responses and adjust their behavior accordingly::\n254: \n255:     if response.streaming:\n256:         response.streaming_content = wrap_streaming_content(response.streaming_content)\n257:     else:\n258:         response.content = alter_content(response.content)\n259: \n260: .. note::\n261: \n262:     ``streaming_content`` should be assumed to be too large to hold in memory.\n263:     Response middleware may wrap it in a new generator, but must not consume\n264:     it. Wrapping is typically implemented as follows::\n265: \n266:         def wrap_streaming_content(content):\n267:             for chunk in content:\n268:                 yield alter_content(chunk)\n269: \n270: :class:`~django.http.StreamingHttpResponse` allows both synchronous and\n271: asynchronous iterators. The wrapping function must match. Check\n272: :attr:`StreamingHttpResponse.is_async\n273: <django.http.StreamingHttpResponse.is_async>` if your middleware needs to\n274: support both types of iterator.\n275: \n276: Exception handling\n277: ==================\n278: \n279: Django automatically converts exceptions raised by the view or by middleware\n280: into an appropriate HTTP response with an error status code. :ref:`Certain\n281: exceptions <error-views>` are converted to 4xx status codes, while an unknown\n282: exception is converted to a 500 status code.\n283: \n284: This conversion takes place before and after each middleware (you can think of\n285: it as the thin film in between each layer of the onion), so that every\n286: middleware can always rely on getting some kind of HTTP response back from\n287: calling its ``get_response`` callable. Middleware don't need to worry about\n288: wrapping their call to ``get_response`` in a ``try/except`` and handling an\n289: exception that might have been raised by a later middleware or the view. Even\n290: if the very next middleware in the chain raises an\n291: :class:`~django.http.Http404` exception, for example, your middleware won't see\n292: that exception; instead it will get an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`\n293: object with a :attr:`~django.http.HttpResponse.status_code` of 404.\n294: \n295: You can set :setting:`DEBUG_PROPAGATE_EXCEPTIONS` to ``True`` to skip this\n296: conversion and propagate exceptions upward.\n297: \n298: .. _async-middleware:\n299: \n300: Asynchronous support\n301: ","url":"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/django/django@202eca7795710c82cd64a248650afc64f905a17e/-/blob/docs/topics/http/middleware.txt%3FL1-300"}}}

Django will adapt requests to fit the middleware's requirements if it\\n305: cannot support both, but at a performance penalty.\\n306: \\n307: By default, Django assumes that your middleware is capable of handling only\\n308: synchronous requests. To change these assumptions, set the following attributes\\n309: on your middleware factory function or class:\\n310: \\n311: * ``sync_capable`` is a boolean indicating if the middleware can handle\\n312:   synchronous requests. Defaults to ``True``.\\n313: \\n314: * ``async_capable`` is a boolean indicating if the middleware can handle\\n315:   asynchronous requests. Defaults to ``False``.\\n316: \\n317: If your middleware has both ``sync_capable = True`` and\\n318: ``async_capable = True``, then Django will pass it the request without\\n319: converting it. In this case, you can work out if your middleware will receive\\n320: async requests by checking if the ``get_response`` object you are passed is a\\n321: coroutine function, using ``inspect.iscoroutinefunction``.\\n322: \\n323: The ``django.utils.decorators`` module contains\\n324: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.sync_only_middleware`,\\n325: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.async_only_middleware`, and\\n326: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.sync_and_async_middleware` decorators that\\n327: allow you to apply these flags to middleware factory functions.\\n328: \\n329: The returned callable must match the sync or async nature of the\\n330: ``get_response`` method. If you have an asynchronous ``get_response``, you must\\n331: return a coroutine function (``async def``).\\n332: \\n333: ``process_view``, ``process_template_response`` and ``process_exception``\\n334: methods, if they are provided, should also be adapted to match the sync/async\\n335: mode. However, Django will individually adapt them as required if you do not,\\n336: at an additional performance penalty.\\n337: \\n338: Here's an example of how to create a middleware function that supports both::\\n339: \\n340:     from inspect import iscoroutinefunction\\n341:     from django.utils.decorators import sync_and_async_middleware\\n342: \\n343: \\n344:     @sync_and_async_middleware\\n345:     def simple_middleware(get_response):\\n346:         # One-time configuration and initialization goes here.\\n347:         if iscoroutinefunction(get_response):\\n348: \\n349:             async def middleware(request):\\n350:                 # Do something here!\\n351:                 response = await get_response(request)\\n352:                 return response\\n353: \\n354:         else:\\n355: \\n356:             def middleware(request):\\n357:                 # Do something here!\\n358:                 response = get_response(request)\\n359:                 return response\\n360: \\n361:         return middleware\\n362: \\n363: .. note::\\n364: \\n365:     If you declare a hybrid middleware that supports both synchronous and\\n366:     asynchronous calls, the kind of call you get may not match the underlying\\n367:     view. Django will optimize the middleware call stack to have as few\\n368:     sync/async transitions as possible.\\n369: \\n370:     Thus, even if you are wrapping an async view, you may be called in sync\\n371:     mode if there is other, synchronous middleware between you and the view.\\n372: \\n373: When using an asynchronous class-based middleware, you must ensure that\\n374: instances are correctly marked as coroutine functions::\\n375: \\n376:     from inspect import iscoroutinefunction, markcoroutinefunction\\n377: \\n378: \\n379:     class AsyncMiddleware:\\n380:         async_capable = True\\n381:         sync_capable = False\\n382: \\n383:         def __init__(self, get_response):\\n384:             self.get_response = get_response\\n385:             if iscoroutinefunction(self.get_response):\\n386:                 markcoroutinefunction(self)\\n387: \\n388:         async def __call__(self, request):\\n389:             response = await self.get_response(request)\\n390:             # Some logic ...\\n391:             return response\\n392: \\n393: .. _upgrading-middleware:\\n394: \\n395: Upgrading pre-Django 1.10-style middleware\\n396: ==========================================\\n397: \\n398: .. class:: django.utils.deprecation.MiddlewareMixin\\n399:     :module:\\n400: \\n401: Django provides ``django.utils.deprecation.MiddlewareMixin`` to ease creating\\n402: middleware classes that are compatible with both :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` and the\\n403: old ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, and support synchronous and asynchronous requests.\\n404: All middleware classes included with Django are compatible with both settings.\\n405: \\n406: The mixin provides an ``__init__()`` method that requires a ``get_response``\\n407: argument and stores it in ``self.get_response``.\\n408: \\n409: The ``__call__()`` method:\\n410: \\n411: #. Calls ``self.process_request(request)`` (if defined).\\n412: #. Calls ``self.get_response(request)`` to get the response from later\\n413:    middleware and the view.\\n414: #. Calls ``self.process_response(request, response)`` (if defined).\\n415: #. Returns the response.\\n416: \\n417: If used with ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, the ``__call__()`` method will\\n418: never be used; Django calls ``process_request()`` and ``process_response()``\\n419: directly.\\n420: \\n421: In most cases, inheriting from this mixin will be sufficient to make an\\n422: old-style middleware compatible with the new system with sufficient\\n423: backwards-compatibility. The new short-circuiting semantics will be harmless or\\n424: even beneficial to the existing middleware. In a few cases, a middleware class\\n425: may need some changes to adjust to the new semantics.\\n426: \\n427: These are the behavioral differences between using :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` and\\n428: ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``:\\n429: \\n430: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, every middleware will always have its\\n431:    ``process_response`` method called, even if an earlier middleware\\n432:    short-circuited by returning a response from its ``process_request``\\n433:    method. Under :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, middleware behaves more like an onion:\\n434:    the layers that a response goes through on the way out are the same layers\\n435:    that saw the request on the way in. If a middleware short-circuits, only\\n436:    that middleware and the ones before it in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` will see the\\n437:    response.\\n438: \\n439: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, ``process_exception`` is applied to\\n440:    exceptions raised from a middleware ``process_request`` method. Under\\n441:    :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, ``process_exception`` applies only to exceptions\\n442:    raised from the view (or from the ``render`` method of a\\n443:    :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse`). Exceptions raised from\\n444:    a middleware are converted to the appropriate HTTP response and then passed\\n445:    to the next middleware.\\n446: \\n447: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, if a ``process_response`` method raises\\n448:    an exception, the ``process_response`` methods of all earlier middleware are\\n449:    skipped and a ``500 Internal Server Error`` HTTP response is always\\n450:    returned (even if the exception raised was e.g. an\\n451:    :class:`~django.http.Http404`). Under :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, an exception\\n452:    raised from a middleware will immediately be converted to the appropriate\\n453:    HTTP response, and then the next middleware in line will see that\\n454:    response. Middleware are never skipped due to a middleware raising an\\n455:    exception.\\n456: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/django/django@202eca7795710c82cd64a248650afc64f905a17e/-/blob/docs/topics/http/middleware.txt%3FL300-500\"}"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"0e625766-dd17-43dd-9caa-0fcad3ad0d87","uuid":"eb20d5db-05df-4f0c-a5db-336693ec93d7","tool_use_result":{"content":"{\"content\":\"300: Asynchronous support\\n301: ====================\\n302: \\n303: Middleware can support any combination of synchronous and asynchronous\\n304: requests. Django will adapt requests to fit the middleware's requirements if it\\n305: cannot support both, but at a performance penalty.\\n306: \\n307: By default, Django assumes that your middleware is capable of handling only\\n308: synchronous requests. To change these assumptions, set the following attributes\\n309: on your middleware factory function or class:\\n310: \\n311: * ``sync_capable`` is a boolean indicating if the middleware can handle\\n312:   synchronous requests. Defaults to ``True``.\\n313: \\n314: * ``async_capable`` is a boolean indicating if the middleware can handle\\n315:   asynchronous requests. Defaults to ``False``.\\n316: \\n317: If your middleware has both ``sync_capable = True`` and\\n318: ``async_capable = True``, then Django will pass it the request without\\n319: converting it. In this case, you can work out if your middleware will receive\\n320: async requests by checking if the ``get_response`` object you are passed is a\\n321: coroutine function, using ``inspect.iscoroutinefunction``.\\n322: \\n323: The ``django.utils.decorators`` module contains\\n324: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.sync_only_middleware`,\\n325: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.async_only_middleware`, and\\n326: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.sync_and_async_middleware` decorators that\\n327: allow you to apply these flags to middleware factory functions.\\n328: \\n329: The returned callable must match the sync or async nature of the\\n330: ``get_response`` method. If you have an asynchronous ``get_response``, you must\\n331: return a coroutine function (``async def``).\\n332: \\n333: ``process_view``, ``process_template_response`` and ``process_exception``\\n334: methods, if they are provided, should also be adapted to match the sync/async\\n335: mode. However, Django will individually adapt them as required if you do not,\\n336: at an additional performance penalty.\\n337: \\n338: Here's an example of how to create a middleware function that supports both::\\n339: \\n340:     from inspect import iscoroutinefunction\\n341:     from django.utils.decorators import sync_and_async_middleware\\n342: \\n343: \\n344:     @sync_and_async_middleware\\n345:     def simple_middleware(get_response):\\n346:         # One-time configuration and initialization goes here.\\n347:         if iscoroutinefunction(get_response):\\n348: \\n349:             async def middleware(request):\\n350:                 # Do something here!\\n351:                 response = await get_response(request)\\n352:                 return response\\n353: \\n354:         else:\\n355: \\n356:             def middleware(request):\\n357:                 # Do something here!\\n358:                 response = get_response(request)\\n359:                 return response\\n360: \\n361:         return middleware\\n362: \\n363: .. note::\\n364: \\n365:     If you declare a hybrid middleware that supports both synchronous and\\n366:     asynchronous calls, the kind of call you get may not match the underlying\\n367:     view. Django will optimize the middleware call stack to have as few\\n368:     sync/async transitions as possible.\\n369: \\n370:     Thus, even if you are wrapping an async view, you may be called in sync\\n371:     mode if there is other, synchronous middleware between you and the view.\\n372: \\n373: When using an asynchronous class-based middleware, you must ensure that\\n374: instances are correctly marked as coroutine functions::\\n375: \\n376:     from inspect import iscoroutinefunction, markcoroutinefunction\\n377: \\n378: \\n379:     class AsyncMiddleware:\\n380:         async_capable = True\\n381:         sync_capable = False\\n382: \\n383:         def __init__(self, get_response):\\n384:             self.get_response = get_response\\n385:             if iscoroutinefunction(self.get_response):\\n386:                 markcoroutinefunction(self)\\n387: \\n388:         async def __call__(self, request):\\n389:             response = await self.get_response(request)\\n390:             # Some logic ...\\n391:             return response\\n392: \\n393: .. _upgrading-middleware:\\n394: \\n395: Upgrading pre-Django 1.10-style middleware\\n396: ==========================================\\n397: \\n398: .. class:: django.utils.deprecation.MiddlewareMixin\\n399:     :module:\\n400: \\n401: Django provides ``django.utils.deprecation.MiddlewareMixin`` to ease creating\\n402: middleware classes that are compatible with both :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` and the\\n403: old ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, and support synchronous and asynchronous requests.\\n404: All middleware classes included with Django are compatible with both settings.\\n405: \\n406: The mixin provides an ``__init__()`` method that requires a ``get_response``\\n407: argument and stores it in ``self.get_response``.\\n408: \\n409: The ``__call__()`` method:\\n410: \\n411: #. Calls ``self.process_request(request)`` (if defined).\\n412: #. Calls ``self.get_response(request)`` to get the response from later\\n413:    middleware and the view.\\n414: #. Calls ``self.process_response(request, response)`` (if defined).\\n415: #. Returns the response.\\n416: \\n417: If used with ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, the ``__call__()`` method will\\n418: never be used; Django calls ``process_request()`` and ``process_response()``\\n419: directly.\\n420: \\n421: In most cases, inheriting from this mixin will be sufficient to make an\\n422: old-style middleware compatible with the new system with sufficient\\n423: backwards-compatibility. The new short-circuiting semantics will be harmless or\\n424: even beneficial to the existing middleware. In a few cases, a middleware class\\n425: may need some changes to adjust to the new semantics.\\n426: \\n427: These are the behavioral differences between using :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` and\\n428: ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``:\\n429: \\n430: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, every middleware will always have its\\n431:    ``process_response`` method called, even if an earlier middleware\\n432:    short-circuited by returning a response from its ``process_request``\\n433:    method. Under :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, middleware behaves more like an onion:\\n434:    the layers that a response goes through on the way out are the same layers\\n435:    that saw the request on the way in. If a middleware short-circuits, only\\n436:    that middleware and the ones before it in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` will see the\\n437:    response.\\n438: \\n439: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, ``process_exception`` is applied to\\n440:    exceptions raised from a middleware ``process_request`` method. Under\\n441:    :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, ``process_exception`` applies only to exceptions\\n442:    raised from the view (or from the ``render`` method of a\\n443:    :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse`). Exceptions raised from\\n444:    a middleware are converted to the appropriate HTTP response and then passed\\n445:    to the next middleware.\\n446: \\n447: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, if a ``process_response`` method raises\\n448:    an exception, the ``process_response`` methods of all earlier middleware are\\n449:    skipped and a ``500 Internal Server Error`` HTTP response is always\\n450:    returned (even if the exception raised was e.g. an\\n451:    :class:`~django.http.Http404`). Under :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, an exception\\n452:    raised from a middleware will immediately be converted to the appropriate\\n453:    HTTP response, and then the next middleware in line will see that\\n454:    response. Middleware are never skipped due to a middleware raising an\\n455:    exception.\\n456: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/django/django@202eca7795710c82cd64a248650afc64f905a17e/-/blob/docs/topics/http/middleware.txt%3FL300-500\"}","structuredContent":{"content":"300: Asynchronous support\n301: ====================\n302: \n303: Middleware can support any combination of synchronous and asynchronous\n304: requests. Django will adapt requests to fit the middleware's requirements if it\n305: cannot support both, but at a performance penalty.\n306: \n307: By default, Django assumes that your middleware is capable of handling only\n308: synchronous requests. To change these assumptions, set the following attributes\n309: on your middleware factory function or class:\n310: \n311: * ``sync_capable`` is a boolean indicating if the middleware can handle\n312:   synchronous requests. Defaults to ``True``.\n313: \n314: * ``async_capable`` is a boolean indicating if the middleware can handle\n315:   asynchronous requests. Defaults to ``False``.\n316: \n317: If your middleware has both ``sync_capable = True`` and\n318: ``async_capable = True``, then Django will pass it the request without\n319: converting it. In this case, you can work out if your middleware will receive\n320: async requests by checking if the ``get_response`` object you are passed is a\n321: coroutine function, using ``inspect.iscoroutinefunction``.\n322: \n323: The ``django.utils.decorators`` module contains\n324: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.sync_only_middleware`,\n325: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.async_only_middleware`, and\n326: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.sync_and_async_middleware` decorators that\n327: allow you to apply these flags to middleware factory functions.\n328: \n329: The returned callable must match the sync or async nature of the\n330: ``get_response`` method. If you have an asynchronous ``get_response``, you must\n331: return a coroutine function (``async def``).\n332: \n333: ``process_view``, ``process_template_response`` and ``process_exception``\n334: methods, if they are provided, should also be adapted to match the sync/async\n335: mode. However, Django will individually adapt them as required if you do not,\n336: at an additional performance penalty.\n337: \n338: Here's an example of how to create a middleware function that supports both::\n339: \n340:     from inspect import iscoroutinefunction\n341:     from django.utils.decorators import sync_and_async_middleware\n342: \n343: \n344:     @sync_and_async_middleware\n345:     def simple_middleware(get_response):\n346:         # One-time configuration and initialization goes here.\n347:         if iscoroutinefunction(get_response):\n348: \n349:             async def middleware(request):\n350:                 # Do something here!\n351:                 response = await get_response(request)\n352:                 return response\n353: \n354:         else:\n355: \n356:             def middleware(request):\n357:                 # Do something here!\n358:                 response = get_response(request)\n359:                 return response\n360: \n361:         return middleware\n362: \n363: .. note::\n364: \n365:     If you declare a hybrid middleware that supports both synchronous and\n366:     asynchronous calls, the kind of call you get may not match the underlying\n367:     view. Django will optimize the middleware call stack to have as few\n368:     sync/async transitions as possible.\n369: \n370:     Thus, even if you are wrapping an async view, you may be called in sync\n371:     mode if there is other, synchronous middleware between you and the view.\n372: \n373: When using an asynchronous class-based middleware, you must ensure that\n374: instances are correctly marked as coroutine functions::\n375: \n376:     from inspect import iscoroutinefunction, markcoroutinefunction\n377: \n378: \n379:     class AsyncMiddleware:\n380:         async_capable = True\n381:         sync_capable = False\n382: \n383:         def __init__(self, get_response):\n384:             self.get_response = get_response\n385:             if iscoroutinefunction(self.get_response):\n386:                 markcoroutinefunction(self)\n387: \n388:         async def __call__(self, request):\n389:             response = await self.get_response(request)\n390:             # Some logic ...\n391:             return response\n392: \n393: .. _upgrading-middleware:\n394: \n395: Upgrading pre-Django 1.10-style middleware\n396: ==========================================\n397: \n398: .. class:: django.utils.deprecation.MiddlewareMixin\n399:     :module:\n400: \n401: Django provides ``django.utils.deprecation.MiddlewareMixin`` to ease creating\n402: middleware classes that are compatible with both :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` and the\n403: old ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, and support synchronous and asynchronous requests.\n404: All middleware classes included with Django are compatible with both settings.\n405: \n406: The mixin provides an ``__init__()`` method that requires a ``get_response``\n407: argument and stores it in ``self.get_response``.\n408: \n409: The ``__call__()`` method:\n410: \n411: #. Calls ``self.process_request(request)`` (if defined).\n412: #. Calls ``self.get_response(request)`` to get the response from later\n413:    middleware and the view.\n414: #. Calls ``self.process_response(request, response)`` (if defined).\n415: #. Returns the response.\n416: \n417: If used with ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, the ``__call__()`` method will\n418: never be used; Django calls ``process_request()`` and ``process_response()``\n419: directly.\n420: \n421: In most cases, inheriting from this mixin will be sufficient to make an\n422: old-style middleware compatible with the new system with sufficient\n423: backwards-compatibility. The new short-circuiting semantics will be harmless or\n424: even beneficial to the existing middleware. In a few cases, a middleware class\n425: may need some changes to adjust to the new semantics.\n426: \n427: These are the behavioral differences between using :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` and\n428: ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``:\n429: \n430: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, every middleware will always have its\n431:    ``process_response`` method called, even if an earlier middleware\n432:    short-circuited by returning a response from its ``process_request``\n433:    method. Under :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, middleware behaves more like an onion:\n434:    the layers that a response goes through on the way out are the same layers\n435:    that saw the request on the way in. If a middleware short-circuits, only\n436:    that middleware and the ones before it in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` will see the\n437:    response.\n438: \n439: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, ``process_exception`` is applied to\n440:    exceptions raised from a middleware ``process_request`` method. Under\n441:    :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, ``process_exception`` applies only to exceptions\n442:    raised from the view (or from the ``render`` method of a\n443:    :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse`). Exceptions raised from\n444:    a middleware are converted to the appropriate HTTP response and then passed\n445:    to the next middleware.\n446: \n447: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, if a ``process_response`` method raises\n448:    an exception, the ``process_response`` methods of all earlier middleware are\n449:    skipped and a ``500 Internal Server Error`` HTTP response is always\n450:    returned (even if the exception raised was e.g. an\n451:    :class:`~django.http.Http404`). Under :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, an exception\n452:    raised from a middleware will immediately be converted to the appropriate\n453:    HTTP response, and then the next middleware in line will see that\n454:    response. Middleware are never skipped due to a middleware raising an\n455:    exception.\n456: ","url":"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/django/django@202eca7795710c82cd64a248650afc64f905a17e/-/blob/docs/topics/http/middleware.txt%3FL300-500"}}}

Overview\\n\\nDjango's middleware setting processing is implemented through an onion-pattern architecture where middleware wraps each other in a stack, with request processing occurring from outermost to innermost, and response processing occurring in reverse order.\\n\\n## Core Files and Components\\n\\n### 1. Middleware Stack Construction (django/core/handlers/base.py)\\n\\nThe `BaseHandler.load_middleware()` method (lines 27-104) is responsible for constructing the complete middleware stack:\\n\\n**Key features:**\\n- Iterates through `settings.MIDDLEWARE` in **reverse order** (line 41: `for middleware_path in reversed(settings.MIDDLEWARE)`)\\n- For each middleware, imports the class/function and instantiates it with `get_response` as the argument\\n- Each middleware wraps the previous handler, creating a nested chain\\n- The innermost handler is `convert_exception_to_response(get_response)` (line 39), which wraps the actual view execution\\n- Handles sync/async adaptation via `adapt_method_mode()` to optimize middleware call stacks\\n- Separates view, template_response, and exception middleware into special lists\\n- The final chain is stored in `_middleware_chain` (line 104)\\n\\n**Middleware instantiation pattern:**\\n```python\\nget_response = self._get_response_async if is_async else self._get_response\\nhandler = convert_exception_to_response(get_response)\\nfor middleware_path in reversed(settings.MIDDLEWARE):\\n    middleware = import_string(middleware_path)\\n    mw_instance = middleware(adapted_handler)  # Wraps the previous handler\\n    handler = convert_exception_to_response(mw_instance)  # Wrap with exception handler\\n```\\n\\n### 2. Individual Middleware Wrapping (django/utils/deprecation.py)\\n\\nThe `MiddlewareMixin` class (lines 276-332) provides the standard middleware interface:\\n\\n**Synchronous execution (`__call__` method, lines 303-313):**\\n1. Calls `process_request(request)` if defined\\n2. If process_request returns None, calls `get_response(request)`\\n3. Calls `process_response(request, response)` if defined\\n4. Returns response\\n\\n**Asynchronous execution (`__acall__` method, lines 315-332):**\\n- Same pattern but using `await` and `sync_to_async()` for sync middleware methods\\n\\n**Short-circuit behavior:**\\n- If `process_request()` returns a response, `get_response()` is never called\\n- Only middleware that saw the request will see the response (onion pattern)\\n\\n### 3. Exception Handling (django/core/handlers/exception.py)\\n\\nThe `convert_exception_to_response()` function (lines 25-61) wraps each middleware:\\n\\n**Key features:**\\n- Catches all exceptions raised by `get_response()`\\n- Converts exceptions to appropriate HTTP responses (404, 403, 400, 500)\\n- Returns a response instead of propagating exceptions\\n- Allows each middleware to always receive a response, not an exception\\n- Applied automatically to every middleware layer\\n\\n### 4. Request/Response Processing (django/core/handlers/base.py)\\n\\n**Request phase (`_get_response()`, lines 176-228):**\\n1. Resolves URL to find the view\\n2. Applies view middleware in order (lines 186-191)\\n3. Calls the view\\n4. Applies template_response middleware if response has render() method (lines 210-226)\\n5. Renders the response\\n\\n**Response phase:**\\n- Occurs automatically as each middleware's `__call__` returns from `get_response()`\\n- Each middleware's code after `get_response()` executes in reverse stack order\\n- Only middleware that executed during request phase execute during response phase\\n\\n### 5. Entry Point (django/core/handlers/wsgi.py)\\n\\nThe `WSGIHandler` class (lines 113-144):\\n\\n**Initialization:**\\n- Calls `load_middleware()` in `__init__` (line 118)\\n- Loads and chains all middleware from settings at server startup\\n\\n**Request handling:**\\n- `__call__` method (lines 120-144) creates the WSGIRequest and calls `get_response()`\\n- The call chain then flows through the complete middleware stack\\n\\n## Comparison with WSGI Middleware\\n\\n### Django's Middleware (MIDDLEWARE setting):\\n\\n1. **Onion Pattern Wrapping:**\\n   - Each middleware explicitly wraps the next via the `get_response` callable\\n   - Request phase: outermost → innermost\\n   - Response phase: innermost → outermost (reverse order)\\n   - If a middleware short-circuits (doesn't call `get_response`), inner middleware are skipped\\n\\n2. **Exception Handling:**\\n   - Exceptions are caught and converted to responses at each layer via `convert_exception_to_response`\\n   - Middleware never see exceptions; they see responses with appropriate status codes\\n   - `process_exception()` only handles view exceptions, not middleware exceptions\\n\\n3. **Short-circuit Semantics:**\\n   - Only middleware that execute during request phase execute during response phase\\n   - No middleware skipping due to exceptions\\n   - Onion layers maintain consistency\\n\\n### WSGI Middleware (older MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES):\\n\\n1. **Sequential List Pattern:**\\n   - Middleware maintained as a list\\n   - `process_request` called in order on each middleware\\n   - `process_response` called on **all** middleware regardless of short-circuit\\n   - This difference is documented in docs/topics/http/middleware.txt lines 430-437\\n\\n2. **Exception Behavior:**\\n   - `process_exception` applies to all exceptions including middleware exceptions\\n   - If `process_response` raises exception, all earlier `process_response` methods skipped\\n   - Returns 500 error unconditionally on exception\\n\\n3. **Processing Order:**\\n   - Request: middleware[0] → middleware[1] → ... → view\\n   - Response: all middleware regardless of request flow\\n   - Not a true onion pattern\\n\\n## Key Architectural Differences\\n\\n| Aspect | Django MIDDLEWARE | WSGI MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES |\\n|--------|------------------|----------------------|\\n| **Wrapping Model** | Explicit function wrapping (onion) | Sequential list |\\n| **Short-circuit Response Phase** | Only earlier middleware | All middleware |\\n| **Exception Handling** | Converted to responses at each layer | Applied to middleware exceptions |\\n| **Consistency** | Request and response paths symmetric | Asymmetric paths |\\n| **Sync/Async Support** | Full support with adaptation | Limited/none |\\n\\n## Middleware Stack Execution Flow\\n\\n```\\nWSGI environ\\n    ↓\\nWSGIHandler.__call__()\\n    ↓\\nget_response(request)  [entry to middleware chain]\\n    ↓\\nMiddleware[0] (outermost):\\n    - process_request()\\n    ↓\\n    Middleware[1]:\\n        - process_request()\\n        ↓\\n        ... (more middleware)\\n        ↓\\n        _get_response():\\n            - view_middleware\\n            - call view\\n            - template_response_middleware\\n        ↓\\n    - process_response()  [return from Middleware[1]]\\n    ↓\\n- process_response()  [return from Middleware[0]]\\n    ↓\\nHTTP Response\\n```\\n\\nThis architecture ensures that the onion pattern is maintained: middleware that executes during the request phase automatically executes during the response phase in reverse order, providing symmetric and predictable behavior.\\n\"\n}\n"},"caller":{"type":"direct"}}],"stop_reason":null,"stop_sequence":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":7,"cache_creation_input_tokens":1536,"cache_read_input_tokens":70227,"cache_creation":{"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":0,"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":1536},"output_tokens":1,"service_tier":"standard","inference_geo":"not_available"},"context_management":null},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"0e625766-dd17-43dd-9caa-0fcad3ad0d87","uuid":"f6903627-d58f-4296-b0af-8c6e73a48608"}

I've created a comprehensive answer documenting Django's middleware implementation. Here's a summary of what I found:\n\n## Key Findings\n\n**Core Implementation Files:**\n1. **django/core/handlers/base.py** - The central middleware loading and execution engine\n2. **django/utils/deprecation.py** - The MiddlewareMixin for standard middleware wrapping\n3. **django/core/handlers/exception.py** - Exception-to-response conversion layer\n4. **django/core/handlers/wsgi.py** - WSGI handler entry point\n5. **docs/topics/http/middleware.txt** - Complete middleware documentation\n\n**How Django's Middleware Stack Works:**\n\n1. **Stack Construction**: In `BaseHandler.load_middleware()`, middleware from `settings.MIDDLEWARE` are loaded in **reverse order**, with each one wrapping the next via the `get_response` callable, creating a nested chain.\n\n2. **Onion Pattern**: \n   - Request flows: outermost middleware → innermost → view\n   - Response flows: innermost → outermost middleware (reverse order)\n   - Creates symmetric request/response processing\n\n3. **Key Difference from WSGI**:\n   - **Django MIDDLEWARE**: Only middleware that execute during request phase execute during response phase (true onion pattern)\n   - **WSGI MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES**: All middleware process_response methods called regardless of short-circuit behavior\n\n4. **Exception Handling**: Each middleware layer is wrapped with `convert_exception_to_response()`, which catches exceptions and converts them to HTTP responses. This allows middleware to never see raw exceptions - they always get responses with appropriate status codes.\n\n5. **Short-Circuiting**: If a middleware's `process_request()` returns a response without calling `get_response()`, the response only flows back through middlewares that already executed, maintaining the onion symmetry.\n\nThe answer file has been created at `/workspace/answer.json` with detailed documentation of the middleware architecture, key symbols, and a comprehensive explanation of how it compares to WSGI middleware patterns."}],"stop_reason":null,"stop_sequence":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":6,"cache_creation_input_tokens":3263,"cache_read_input_tokens":71763,"cache_creation":{"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":0,"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":3263},"output_tokens":1,"service_tier":"standard","inference_geo":"not_available"},"context_management":null},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"0e625766-dd17-43dd-9caa-0fcad3ad0d87","uuid":"7f08d646-874f-4039-a081-8771872b84eb"}

I've created a comprehensive answer documenting Django's middleware implementation. Here's a summary of what I found:\n\n## Key Findings\n\n**Core Implementation Files:**\n1. **django/core/handlers/base.py** - The central middleware loading and execution engine\n2. **django/utils/deprecation.py** - The MiddlewareMixin for standard middleware wrapping\n3. **django/core/handlers/exception.py** - Exception-to-response conversion layer\n4. **django/core/handlers/wsgi.py** - WSGI handler entry point\n5. **docs/topics/http/middleware.txt** - Complete middleware documentation\n\n**How Django's Middleware Stack Works:**\n\n1. **Stack Construction**: In `BaseHandler.load_middleware()`, middleware from `settings.MIDDLEWARE` are loaded in **reverse order**, with each one wrapping the next via the `get_response` callable, creating a nested chain.\n\n2. **Onion Pattern**: \n   - Request flows: outermost middleware → innermost → view\n   - Response flows: innermost → outermost middleware (reverse order)\n   - Creates symmetric request/response processing\n\n3. **Key Difference from WSGI**:\n   - **Django MIDDLEWARE**: Only middleware that execute during request phase execute during response phase (true onion pattern)\n   - **WSGI MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES**: All middleware process_response methods called regardless of short-circuit behavior\n\n4. **Exception Handling**: Each middleware layer is wrapped with `convert_exception_to_response()`, which catches exceptions and converts them to HTTP responses. This allows middleware to never see raw exceptions - they always get responses with appropriate status codes.\n\n5. **Short-Circuiting**: If a middleware's `process_request()` returns a response without calling `get_response()`, the response only flows back through middlewares that already executed, maintaining the onion symmetry.\n\nThe answer file has been created at `/workspace/answer.json` with detailed documentation of the middleware architecture, key symbols, and a comprehensive explanation of how it compares to WSGI middleware patterns.","stop_reason":null,"session_id":"0e625766-dd17-43dd-9caa-0fcad3ad0d87","total_cost_usd":0.17319779999999996,"usage":{"input_tokens":134,"cache_creation_input_tokens":48162,"cache_read_input_tokens":829963,"output_tokens":5973,"server_tool_use":{"web_search_requests":0,"web_fetch_requests":0},"service_tier":"standard","cache_creation":{"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":48162,"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":0},"inference_geo":"","iterations":[],"speed":"standard"},"modelUsage":{"claude-haiku-4-5-20251001":{"inputTokens":134,"outputTokens":5973,"cacheReadInputTokens":829963,"cacheCreationInputTokens":48162,"webSearchRequests":0,"costUSD":0.17319779999999996,"contextWindow":200000,"maxOutputTokens":32000}},"permission_denials":[],"fast_mode_state":"off","uuid":"02d5c91a-f73f-463e-b8de-6df240a6fbcd"}
